<p><b>Livorno</b> (<small>Italian: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Italian" title="Help:IPA for Italian">[liˈvorno]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<a href="/wiki/File:It-Livorno.ogg" title="File:It-Livorno.ogg"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a> <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/It-Livorno.ogg" title="It-Livorno.ogg">listen</a>)</small>) is a port city on the <a href="/wiki/Ligurian_Sea" title="Ligurian Sea">Ligurian Sea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> on the western coast of <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> It is the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Livorno" title="Province of Livorno">Province of Livorno</a>, having a population of 159,431 residents in February 2015. It has traditionally been known in English as <b>Leghorn</b>, pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ɔːr/ 'or' in 'abhor'">ɔːr</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i>leg-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AWRN</span></span></b></i></a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cham20_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cham20-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collins-concise_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-collins-concise-5">[5]</a></sup> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɔːr/ 'or' in 'abhor'">ɔːr</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">LEG</span></span></b>-hawrn</i></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxf_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxf-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Livorno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Livorno" title="Timeline of Livorno">Timeline of Livorno</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="653" data-file-width="995" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg/220px-Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg/330px-Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg/440px-Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Livorno_map_17th_Century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fortifications of Livorno in the 17th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Livorno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The origin of Livorno is dubious, although the place was inhabited since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> Age as shown by worked bones, pieces of copper and ceramic found on the <a href="/wiki/Livorno_Hills" title="Livorno Hills">Livorno Hills</a> in a cave between Ardenza and Montenero. The construction of the <a href="/wiki/Via_Aurelia" title="Via Aurelia">Via Aurelia</a> coincided with the occupation of the region by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, who left traces of their presence in the <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">toponyms</a> and ruins of towers. The natural cove called <i>Liburna</i>, later transformed in Livorna then in Livorno, is a reference to the type of ship, the <a href="/wiki/Liburna" title="Liburna">liburna</a>, used by <a href="/wiki/Roman_navy" title="Roman navy">Roman navy</a>. Others ancient toponyms include: <i>Salviano</i> (Salvius), <i>Antignano</i> (Ante ignem) which was the place situated before <i>Ardenza</i> (Ardentia) where were the beacons for the ships directed to <a href="/wiki/Porto_Pisano" title="Porto Pisano">Porto Pisano</a>.</p>
<p>The name <i>Livorna</i> is mentioned for the first time in 1017 as a small coastal village, the port and the remains of a Roman tower. In 1077 a tower was built by <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Tuscany" title="Matilda of Tuscany">Matilda of Tuscany</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Pisa" title="Republic of Pisa">Republic of Pisa</a> possessed Livorno from 1103 and built there a quadrangular Fort called <i>Quadratura dei Pisani</i> ("Quarter of the Pisans") in defence of the port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treccanilivorno_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-treccanilivorno-7">[7]</a></sup> <i>Porto Pisano</i> was destroyed after the crushing defeat of the Pisan fleet in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Meloria_(1284)" title="Battle of Meloria (1284)">Battle of Meloria</a> in 1284.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Livorno was bought in 1399 by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Visconti#Visconti_of_Milan" title="House of Visconti">Visconti of Milan</a>, then was sold to the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a> in 1405 and afterwards was bought definitively from <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic of Florence</a> on August 28, 1421.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treccanilivorno_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-treccanilivorno-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1427 and 1429, the census was held. According to the results of the census, there were 118 families in Livorno, which made 423 persons. Monks, Jews, military personnel, and the homeless were not included in the census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1551 the population was 1562 residents, in 1745 it had risen to 32,534 and in 1861 at the unification was 96,471 inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treccanilivorno_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-treccanilivorno-7">[7]</a></sup> The only remainder of medieval Livorno is a fragment of two towers and a wall, located inside the <a href="/wiki/Livorno#Fortezza_Vecchia" title="Livorno">Fortezza Vecchia</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicean_period">Medicean period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Livorno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medicean period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici</a>, the ruling dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, some modifications were made, in particular, the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Fortress,_Livorno" title="Old Fortress, Livorno">Fortezza Vecchia</a></i> was constructed between 1518 and 1534, and the voluntary resettlement of the population to Livorno was stimulated, but Livorno still remained a rather insignificant coastal fortress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Livorno was designed as an "<a href="/wiki/Ideal_town" title="Ideal town">Ideal town</a>" during the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, when it was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Tuscany" title="Grand Duchy of Tuscany">Grand Duchy of Tuscany</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">House of Medici</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treccanilivorno_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-treccanilivorno-7">[7]</a></sup> The first plan was drawn by the architect <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Buontalenti" title="Bernardo Buontalenti">Bernardo Buontalenti</a> in 1577,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treccanilivorno_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-treccanilivorno-7">[7]</a></sup> the new fortified town had a <a href="/wiki/Pentagon" title="Pentagon">pentagon</a> plant, for this reason is called <i><a href="/wiki/Livorno#Pentagono_del_Buontalenti" title="Livorno">Pentagono del Buontalenti</a></i>, incorporating the original settlement. The <i><a href="/wiki/Port_of_Livorno#Porto_Mediceo" title="Port of Livorno">Porto Mediceo</a></i> was overlooked and defended by <a href="/wiki/Tower" title="Tower">towers</a> and fortresses leading to the <a href="/wiki/Town_centre" title="Town centre">town centre</a>.</p>
<p>In the late 1580s, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinando_I_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Ferdinando I de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Ferdinando I de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a>, declared Livorno a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_free_ports" title="List of free ports">free port</a> (<i>porto franco</i>), which meant that the goods traded here were <a href="/wiki/Duty-free_shop" title="Duty-free shop">duty-free</a> within the area of the town's control. To regulate this trade, in 1593 the Duke's administration established the <i>Leggi Livornine</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-treccanilivorno_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-treccanilivorno-7">[7]</a></sup> These laws were in force until 1603, until the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>. The laws established a well-regulated market, protecting merchant activities from crime and <a href="/wiki/Racket_(crime)" title="Racket (crime)">racketeering</a>, and instituted laws regarding <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>.</p>
<p>Additionally, expanding Christian tolerance, the laws offered the right of public <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amnesty" title="Amnesty">amnesty</a> to people having to gain <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a> given by clergy in order to conduct civil business. The Grand Duke attracted numerous Turks, Persians, Moors, Greeks, and Armenians, along with <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> immigrants, beginning in the late sixteenth century with the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra_Decree" title="Alhambra Decree">Alhambra Decree</a>, which resulted in the expulsion from Spain and Portugal, and extended them rights and privileges; they contributed to the mercantile wealth and scholarship in the city.</p>
<p>Livorno became an enlightened European city and one of the most important ports of the entire <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>. Many European <a href="/wiki/Alien_(law)" title="Alien (law)">foreigners</a> moved to Livorno. These included Christian Protestant reformers who supported such leaders as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, and others. <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a>, and English arrived, along with Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>. Meanwhile, Jews continued to trade under their previous treaties with the Grand Duke. On 19 March 1606, Ferdinando I de' Medici elevated Livorno to the rank of city; the ceremony was held in the <i>Fortezza Vecchia</i> Chapel of <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">Francis of Assisi</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chiesa_Santa_Caterina,_Livorno.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3227" data-file-width="2431" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Chiesa_Santa_Caterina%2C_Livorno.jpg/200px-Chiesa_Santa_Caterina%2C_Livorno.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Chiesa_Santa_Caterina%2C_Livorno.jpg/300px-Chiesa_Santa_Caterina%2C_Livorno.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Chiesa_Santa_Caterina%2C_Livorno.jpg/400px-Chiesa_Santa_Caterina%2C_Livorno.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chiesa_Santa_Caterina,_Livorno.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Santa_Caterina_(Livorno)" title="Santa Caterina (Livorno)">Santa Caterina, Livorno</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> increased tensions among Christians; dissidents to the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Papacy</a> were targeted by various Catholic absolute rulers. Livorno's tolerance fell victim to the <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion" title="European wars of religion">European wars of religion</a>. But, in the preceding period, the merchants of Livorno had developed a series of trading networks with Protestant Europe, and the Dutch, British, and Germans worked to retain these.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_century_and_later">17th century and later</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Livorno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 17th century and later">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the end of the 17th century, Livorno underwent a period of great <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> and expansion. Near the defensive pile of the Old Fortress, a new fortress was built, together with the town walls and the system of navigable canals through neighborhoods. After the port of <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> silted up in XIII° century, its distance from the sea was increased and it lost its dominance in trade. Livorno took over as the main port in <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>.</p>
<p>The more successful of the European powers re-established trading houses in the region, especially the British with the <a href="/wiki/Levant_Company" title="Levant Company">Levant Company</a>. In turn, the trading networks grew, and with it, Britain's cultural contact with Tuscany. An increasing number of British writers, artists, philosophers, and travelers visited the area and developed the unique historical ties between the two communities. The British referred to the city as Leghorn.</p>
<p>Through the centuries, the city's trade fortunes fell and rose according to the success or failure of the Great Powers. The British and their Protestant allies were important to its trade.</p>
<p>The first American-born saint, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Ann_Seton" title="Elizabeth Ann Seton">Elizabeth Ann Seton</a> converted from Protestantism to Catholicism while visiting Italian friends in Livorno in the early 19th century.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Livorno_map_of_the_town_(1849)_by_Guesdon_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2267" data-file-width="2536" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Livorno_map_of_the_town_%281849%29_by_Guesdon_01.jpg/300px-Livorno_map_of_the_town_%281849%29_by_Guesdon_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Livorno_map_of_the_town_%281849%29_by_Guesdon_01.jpg/450px-Livorno_map_of_the_town_%281849%29_by_Guesdon_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Livorno_map_of_the_town_%281849%29_by_Guesdon_01.jpg/600px-Livorno_map_of_the_town_%281849%29_by_Guesdon_01.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Livorno_map_of_the_town_(1849)_by_Guesdon_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bird's-eye view of Livorno in mid 19th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> of the early nineteenth century, the French prohibited trade with Britain, and the economy of Livorno suffered greatly. The French had taken over Tuscany in 1808, incorporating it into their empire. In 1861, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> succeeded in its wars of unification, and Livorno and <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a> became part of the new <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a>. Livorno lost its status as a free port and the city's commercial importance declined.</p>
<p>In the 18th and 19th centuries, Livorno had numerous <a href="/wiki/Urban_park" title="Urban park">public parks</a> housing important museums as <i><a href="/wiki/Livorno#Museo_Civico_Giovanni_Fattori" title="Livorno">Museo Civico Giovanni Fattori</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Livorno#Museo_di_storia_naturale_del_Mediterraneo" title="Livorno">Museo di storia naturale del Mediterraneo</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_institution" title="Cultural institution">cultural institutions</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Livorno#Biblioteca_Labronica_F.D._Guerrazzi" title="Livorno">Biblioteca Labronica F.D. Guerrazzi</a></i> and other in <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical style</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Cisternoni_of_Livorno#La_Gran_Conserva" title="Cisternoni of Livorno">Cisternone</a></i>, <i>Teatro Goldoni</i> and <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Liberty style</a> as <i>Palazzo Corallo</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Market_(place)" title="Market (place)">Mercato</a> delle Vettovaglie</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">Stabilimento termale Acque</a> della Salute</i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Primary_education#Italy" title="Primary education">Scuole elementari</a> Benci</i> all the last on project by Angiolo Badaloni. In the 1930s were built numerous <a href="/wiki/Villa" title="Villa">villas</a> on the avenue along the sea in <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Liberty style</a> on design by Cioni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Livorno suffered extensive damage during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Many historic sites and buildings were destroyed by bombs of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> preceding their invasion, including the <a href="/wiki/Livorno#Cathedral_of_Saint_Francis_of_Assisi" title="Livorno">cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synagogue_of_Livorno" title="Synagogue of Livorno">the synagogue</a>. Livorno's citizens in recent decades have become well known for their <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing politics</a>. The Italian communist party was founded in Livorno in 1921.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Livorno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Livorno has a hot-summer <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">mediterranean climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>Csa</i>). Summers have warm days with the heat lingering on throughout the night, hence going above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_climate" title="Subtropical climate">subtropical</a> threshold in spite of its relatively high latitude. Winters are mild for the latitude due to the influence from the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. Precipitation is in a wet winter/dry summer-pattern as with all climates fitting the mediterranean definition.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Livorno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation">
